11/5/22, 3:35 PM - Akshat Cycling: int n=logs.size();

for(int i=0;i<n;i++)
{
	String s=logs.get(i);
	String inp[]=s.split(" ");
	String type=inp[0];
	String uname=inp[1];
	String pass= inp.size==3?inp[2]:"";
	
	if(type.equals("register"))
	{
		if(hm.containsKey("uname"))
		{
			ans.add("Username already exists");
		}
		else
		{
			hm.put(uname, new String[]{pass,"0"});
			ans.add("Registered Successfully");
		}
	}
	else if(type.equals("login"))
	{
		if(!hm.containsKey(uname))
		{
			ans.add("Login Unsuccessful");
		}
		else
		{
			String curr[]=hm.get(uname);
			
			if(curr[1].equals("1") || !curr[0].equals(pass))
			{
				ans.add("Login Unsuccessful");
			}
			else
			{
				hm.put(uname, new String[]{curr[0],"1"});
				ans.add("Logged In Successfully");
			}
		}
	}
	else if(type.equals("logout"))
	{
		if(!hm.containsKey(uname))
		{
			ans.add("Logout Unsuccessful");
		}
		else
		{
			String curr[]=hm.get(uname);
			if(curr[1].equals("0"))
			{
				ans.add("Logout Unsuccessful");
			}
			else
			{
				String curr[]=hm.get(uname);
				if(curr[1].equals("0"))
				{
					ans.add("Logout Unsuccessful");
				}
				else
				{
					hm.put(uname, new String[]{curr[0],"0"});
					ans.add("Logout Successful");
				}
			}
		}
	}
}
}

11/5/22, 9:31 PM - Devasy: #include <stdio.h>
int main()
{
    int A[100][4]; // Matrix for storing Process Id, Burst
                   // Time, Average Waiting Time & Average
                   // Turn Around Time.
    int i, j, n, total = 0, index, temp;
    float avg_wt, avg_tat;
    printf("Enter number of process: ");
    scanf("%d", &n);
    printf("Enter Burst Time:\n");
    // User Input Burst Time and alloting Process Id.
    for (i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        printf("P%d: ", i + 1);
        scanf("%d", &A[i][1]);
        A[i][0] = i + 1;
    }
    // Sorting process according to their Burst Time.
    for (i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        index = i;
        for (j = i + 1; j < n; j++)
            if (A[j][1] < A[index][1])
                index = j;
        temp = A[i][1];
        A[i][1] = A[index][1];
        A[index][1] = temp;
 
        temp = A[i][0];
        A[i][0] = A[index][0];
        A[index][0] = temp;
    }
    A[0][2] = 0;
    // Calculation of Waiting Times
    for (i = 1; i < n; i++) {
        A[i][2] = 0;
        for (j = 0; j < i; j++)
            A[i][2] += A[j][1];
        total += A[i][2];
    }
    avg_wt = (float)total / n;
    total = 0;
    printf("P     BT     WT     TAT\n");
    // Calculation of Turn Around Time and printing the
    // data.
    for (i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        A[i][3] = A[i][1] + A[i][2];
        total += A[i][3];
        printf("P%d     %d     %d      %d\n", A[i][0],
               A[i][1], A[i][2], A[i][3]);
    }
    avg_tat = (float)total / n;
    printf("Average Waiting Time= %f", avg_wt);
    printf("\nAverage Turnaround Time= %f", avg_tat);
}
11/5/22, 9:35 PM - Devasy: // round robin scheduling
#include <iostream>
#include <vector>
#include <algorithm>
#include <queue>
using namespace std;
int main()
{
    int n, q;
    cin >> n >> q;
    vector<int> v(n);
    queue<int> q1;
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++)
    {
        cin >> v[i];
        q1.push(v[i]);
    }
    int time = 0;
    while (!q1.empty())
    {
        int temp = q1.front();
        q1.pop();
        if (temp > q)
        {
            temp -= q;
            time += q;
            q1.push(temp);
        }
        else
        {
            time += temp;
            cout << time << endl;
        }
    }
    return 0;
}

11/5/22, 9:39 PM - Devasy: // Write a program to stimulate deadlock in the system using threads. (Hint: Use Mutex-
// Locks).
#include <bits/stdc++.h>
#include <pthread.h>
using namespace std;
pthread_mutex_t lock1 = PTHREAD_MUTEX_INITIALIZER;
pthread_mutex_t lock2 = PTHREAD_MUTEX_INITIALIZER;
void *thread1(void *arg)
{
    pthread_mutex_lock(&lock1);
    cout << "Thread 1 acquired lock 1" << endl;
    sleep(1);
    cout << "Thread 1 waiting for lock 2" << endl;
    pthread_mutex_lock(&lock2);
    cout << "Thread 1 acquired lock 2" << endl;
    pthread_mutex_unlock(&lock1);
    pthread_mutex_unlock(&lock2);
}
void *thread2(void *arg)
{
    pthread_mutex_lock(&lock2);
    cout << "Thread 2 acquired lock 2" << endl;
    sleep(1);
    cout << "Thread 2 waiting for lock 1" << endl;
    pthread_mutex_lock(&lock1);
    cout << "Thread 2 acquired lock 1" << endl;
    pthread_mutex_unlock(&lock1);
    pthread_mutex_unlock(&lock2);
}
int main()
{
    pthread_t t1, t2;
    pthread_create(&t1, NULL, thread1, NULL);
    pthread_create(&t2, NULL, thread2, NULL);
    pthread_join(t1, NULL);
    pthread_join(t2, NULL);
    return 0;
}

}

	return 0;
}

11/10/22, 10:36 PM - Devasy: V.  !" 
Explicit and Implicit solutions can be compared based on the
following parameters:
1) User Interaction: It refers to how much important it is
for the system to interact with the users to learn about them.
2) User Effort: It refers to how much work the user has to
do to help the system understand his/her preferences. Work is
usually to rate some given items or answer questions. If the user
feels overwhelmed by the work, he or she may leave the system.
3) Confidence: It implies, how much the system can
improve itself by learning from user’s feedbacks during the
recommendation process [18].
4) Relevance: It refers to how much informative data can be
obtained.
5) Exploration-Exploitation dilemma: It indicates how the
system has to choose between recommending an item that
allows it to learn more (explore) about the user or an item that
keeps the user interested (exploit) [19].
6) Privacy: Privacy settings, which allow users to control
the information they share, may restrict the information
accessed by the system. It refers to how much the system is
restricted by privacy settings.
In Table 1, comparisons are made between the two classes of
solutions based on the above described parameters. These
parameters affect the quality of the recommendations in the
long run and contrast the functional aspects of the two sets of
solutions.
From the comparison, the following conclusions can be
drawn. Since confidence and user effort are related to level of
user interaction, explicit solutions have better scope for
improvement as the recommendation process progresses, but
require considerable user effort and can thus overwhelm the
user. On the other hand, implicit solutions may require lesser
user effort, but have limited growth of confidence. They
however do not face the exploration-exploitation dilemma,
which explicit solutions can face. An implication of this
dilemma is that the system may need to restrict its learning
process after a certain point of time, since recommending
excessive test items reduce overall quality of the
recommendations and tests the user’s patience, which is
unacceptable. When it comes to privacy, implicit solutions
could face restrictions. Thus, a researcher has to make a tradeoff
between the benefits and shortcomings while choosing
between one of these approaches.

One such model is called a StyleGAN, which is capable of generating high-resolution images that can be used as wallpapers.

Here is an example of how you might use a StyleGAN model to generate wallpapers based on 100 input images of resolution (1920 x 1080):

First, you would need to gather and pre-process your input images. This might involve resizing the images to match the desired resolution (1920 x 1080), and converting them to a format that the StyleGAN model can understand (such as JPEG or PNG).

Next, you would need to train the StyleGAN model on your input images. This typically involves using a large amount of computational power and can take several hours or even days to complete, depending on the size and complexity of your dataset.

Once the model is trained, you can use it to generate new wallpapers by providing it with a random noise vector and letting it generate an image based on the input images it was trained on.

You can repeat this process multiple times to generate a large number of unique wallpapers, each with its own distinct style and appearance.

Overall, generating wallpapers using a GAN model is a complex process that involves a combination of machine learning techniques, large amounts of data, and significant computational power. However, the results can be impressive, and GAN-generated wallpapers can be used to create unique and visually appealing designs.
